A New Window to the Canal To reaffirm the commitment to keep the population informed on what is happening in the Canal, the Panama Canal officially launched Tu Canal TV, a free signal TV channel that constitutes a new bridge of communication between the waterway and the community. The channel airs 24-hours a day, seven days a week, with a high definition digital free signal in Channel 26 and coverage in the provinces of Panama, West Panama and Colon. In addition, Tu Canal TV is also included as part of the cable + TV Digital and Cableonda signals, to achieve better national coverage. Furthermore, Tu Canal TV also airs live the main milestones of the Panama Canal Expansion and other events in the waterway that are important to national life, for the population to be able to follow first-hand. 188 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 Positioning of Digital Communication The Panama Canal digital communication platform consists of our websites and social media presence. It was used to position the company’s messaging with regard to the progress of the Panama Canal Expansion, including several activities and events that took place throughout the year. The promotion of public relations efforts was mainly done through social media, the Panama Canal website, and mobile applications. This strategy helped to strengthen the company’s positioning in this type of communication, as evidenced by the growing number of followers in the Canal’s social media channels, which increased 75 percent, from 250,000 followers in fiscal 2014 to more than 437,000 in fiscal 2015. International Communication International communication in the Panama Canal was particularly important in areas such as communication of the new toll structure and organization of the public hearing on that topic. During fiscal 2015, the communication strategy was aimed at strengthening the international reputation of the Panama Canal Expansion and the Canal as a reliable, safe and efficient route. Similarly, an international communication strategy was elaborated to raise awareness on the major milestones of the Expansion Program and training for its operation, in order to keep the international maritime community informed about its progress and opening. Crisis Management Since the contractual dispute related to the construction of the Third Set of Locks and the negotiation of the Collective Bargaining Agreements, the Vice-Presidency of Corporate Communications was responsible for managing the corporate messaging of the Panama Canal between several interest groups, main international and national media outlets, and civil society groups in order to communicate to the public in a timely manner. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 189 Environmental Management Environmental Management The Canal administration promotes environmental management in both, operations areas and the Canal Watershed as a part of its strategy “Panama, The Green Route of the World”. This strategy supports sustainable development, considering the balance between economic progress, population growth, rational use of resources, protection and conservation of water resources, and the environment. Due to climatic variability event El Niño, rainfall and water input to the Canal reservoirs decreased in fiscal year 2015. This highlighted the importance of programs and initiatives for water management and conservation, for the benefit of populations living around the Canal Watershed and at main cities of Colon, Panama, La Chorrera and Arraijan, which comprise more than 55 percent of the country’s population. Through inter-agency coordination, the Panama Canal is developing a water management strategy that promotes participation and concurrence of the different stakeholders that interact in the Watershed (communities, institutions, NGOs and local authorities, among others). Also, the cadastre and land titling initiatives provide the population with the legal ownership of their land; while the Environmental Economic Incentives Program (EEIP) works with farmers in generating economic resources and improving their quality of life while protecting natural resources. Moreover, a policy of open dialogue with organizations continues to facilitate local and regional spaces for community participation. The Panama Canal operation complies with the strictest environmental regulations, through which it guarantees a clean, sustainable and environmentally-friendly operation. To do this, the corporate environmental management promotes cultural changes that can only be achieved through environmental education, practice and monitoring. For instance, it has successfully developed a Water Quality Surveillance and Monitoring program, as well as a potable Water Sampling program, among others. The Panama Canal reached one hundred years of supplying drinking water, thanks to its human resources and maintenance plans, constant innovations and process improvements. Moreover, it remains at the forefront of energy production, and guarantees quality and continuity of electrical energy supply for Canal operation. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 191 Watershed Conservation The Panama Canal Watershed covers about 339,000 hectares of land, divided into 42 townships and 7 districts across the provinces of Panama and Colon. It is one of the most important areas for the Republic of Panama. Natural elements, vegetation cover and rich ecosystems are some of the features handled by conservation and environmental restoration programs the Canal carries out in close collaboration with different stakeholders. There is a close relationship between water, soil, forests, Watershed communities and the Panama Canal, which contributes to environmental conservation and the welfare of all. Forest protection is not limited to keeping trees; it goes further and focuses on preserving an ecosystem in which there are still many elements to discover. Also, the proper use of water resources is vital to tackle problems associated with climate change and El Niño, that have caused historic low levels of rainfall in the country and Watershed during fiscal year 2015, and which are expected to last until April 2016. Green Initiatives The Green Route In March 2015, the Green Route Inter-departmental Committee (CIRV) approved and published the Green Route Strategy on the Panama Canal website. The strategy includes three lines of action: a) low-carbon operation, b) socio-environmental management; and c) emission reduction and environmental management. The document highlights the contributions of the Canal to global efforts to mitigate climate change, by offering the world a shorter route through which customers are able to reduce greenhouse gases (GHG) emissions, compared to alternative routes. In June 2015, the study “Identification of Aspects to Add Value to the Panama Canal Route” was completed as a result of the Non-Reimbursable Technical Cooperation Agreement between the 192 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 Latin America Development Bank (CAF) and the Panama Canal. The study proposed an incentive scheme designed to reward customers for their environmental performance. Currently, incentives and compensation practices are being analyzed, considering actions such as the use of more efficient engines and the application of best environmental practices beyond the limits set by the International Maritime Organization (IMO), and compliance with internationally recognized standards. The Panama Canal Watershed stores approximately 141 million tons of carbon in forests, bushes and different vegetation cover. The announcement came after days of fieldwork; data collection on forests and vegetation cover types; satellite imagery interpretation; statistical analysis; modelling using geographic information systems, and mapping. To quantify the carbon stored in the Watershed, the Panama Canal and the German Society for International Cooperation (GIZ) worked together during fiscal years 2014 and 2015 to complete the inventory of natural forests, reforested areas under the Environmental Economic Incentives Program (EEIP) and shrub-covered areas. The results show that 90 percent of carbon is stored in forests, which cover 57 percent of the Watershed territory. A total of 6.2 percent is stored in crops and pasture production systems, which cover 21 percent of the territory. The rest is stored in areas covered by bushes and plantations, either unmixed or in agroforestry systems. Vegetation Cover Monitoring in the Watershed The 2014 Environmental Situation Report of the Panama Canal Watershed was published in fiscal year 2015, presenting the progress made on natural resources administration and integrated management. The report on the Panama Canal Watershed Vegetation Cover and Land Use was also published, offering an analysis of dynamic change of vegetation and land use. As part of the Monitoring Program for the Panama Canal Watershed Vegetation Cover, 15 overflights were conducted jointly with the Ministry of Environment. This information helps to complement the satellite image interpretation and decision making process for forests management and protection. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 193 Environmental Economic Incentives Program For the first time, during fiscal year 2015 the Canal established a direct incentive mechanism for protecting forests in the Watershed. This is part of the forest protection and surveillance component of the EEIP. Throughout the period, 87 farms covering 600 hectares were certified in compliance with the program; 37 certificates and incentives were granted. In addition, the Panama Canal continued planting this year 202 hectares of tree and shrub species, 32 of which correspond to agroforestry plots with coffee and cocoa, and 150 of them were a combination of trees and pastures. A total of 20 hectares of traditional coffee farms were improved. The Association of Coffee Producers of the Ciri and Trinidad River Basins at the Panama Canal (ACACPA), located in the district of Capira, achieved a sale of 1,000 coffee sacks –of 45 kilograms eachduring the 2015 harvest, with support and technical assistance from the Panama Canal. This was the first time these coffee farmers made a sale through the association they founded in 2012, thus generating benefits for them, their families, and an economic stimulus for the region. On the other hand the Canal, in coordination with the Land Management National Authority (ANATI) continues the program of Cadastre and Land Titling in the Watershed. It has registered 43,566 hectares to date in the district of Capira and has extended the scope of the program to the district of La Chorrera. An estimated of 5,272 farmers and people living in the Watershed have obtained their land property title: 3,669 men, 1,569 women and 34 legal persons. In 2015, ANATI issued resolution ADMG096-2015 of May 20, 2015 which states that rural areas of Ciri Grande, Ciri de los Sotos and Santa Rosa, in the district of Capira, province of Panama -all of them in the Canal Watershed -were a Regularized Zone. The Cadastre and Land Titling Program is the foundation for establishing activities of the EEIP, and strengthens long-term sustainable use of water resources in the Watershed, giving local inhabitants the opportunity to improve their living conditions. Environmental Education and Community Relations On integrated management of water resources, community involvement is key. This approach to water governance in the Canal Watershed stands on a local participatory structure that includes six consultative councils and twenty six local committees. This participation of multiple stakeholders made possible the implementation of the Project for Strengthening and Monitoring Local Committees 194 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 and Consultative Councils; training on strategic themes was offered, such as organization’s bylaws, the Green Route, and the Land Use General Law for the Interoceanic Region (Law 21 of July 2, 1997). In addition, interagency and community-based organizations were assisted with the implementation of the Solid Waste Management Program in the model community of Nuevo Progreso, initiating a scheme for integrated management of solid waste at the lower section of Chilibrillo River. This program is facilitating preservation of water resources, with participation of institutions, local governments, private sector and the community. Furthermore, through the Environmental Conservation and Vocational Training Program carried out by the Canal and the National Institute of Professional Education and Training for Human Development (INADEH), it was possible to train 375 participants with environmental expertise as well as occupational skills. During fiscal year 2015, 153 schools, 35,000 students and 2,000 teachers were reached through formal education initiatives in the sub-Watersheds of the Chilibre and Chilibrillo rivers and the Chagres-Alhajuela area. Another 400 Watershed Guardian students were reached through the Solid Waste Management Program. In addition, the Environmental Events Program offered workshops on reuse of materials, carried out a mural contest in commemoration of the 101 years of the Canal with the slogan “A Clean Canal,” and organized an environmental festival with the slogan “Rivers of Life, Not Waste,” with the participation of 390 students. In the framework of Our Canal and its Watershed Program, the Panama Canal continued to encourage entrepreneurial awareness, solidarity and respect for the environment with the participation of 2,190 students in 50 schools. In 2015, a round table for dialog and consensus was set among the Consultative Councils of ChilibreChililibrillo and Chagres-Alhajuela; the Committee for the Defense of the Chagres National Park and Lake Alhajuela; and delegates from member institutions of the Permanent Technical Committee of the Inter-institutional Commission for the Panama Canal Watershed (CICH)1. The objective was to reach agreements on alternatives regarding mining concessions approved in areas close to the Chagres National Park. 1The CICH supervises and evaluates programs, projects and policies for the adequate management of the Watershed. It resolves possible inconsistencies or duplicate efforts among stakeholders. This commission has its legal foundation in Article 6 of Law 19 of June 11, 1997 of the Organic Law which creates the ACP. The commission is regulated under Agreement No. 116 (July 27, 2006) Chapter VII of the Board of Directors of the ACP. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 195 The Panama Canal, which heads the CICH, works to ensure an integrated Watershed management, implementing programs, projects and activities aimed at the management of water resources and improving living conditions of its inhabitants. Notably, it has based its water governance strategy on the participation and consultation with the multiple stakeholders that interact within the Watershed territory. In fiscal year 2015, through the CICH, members of its Permanent Technical Committee completed several field inspections to monitor and determine compliance on activities that might have an impact in this area. Additionally, the Administrator of the Panama Canal and the Minister of Health signed a cooperation agreement to ensure drinking water supply to rural water distribution systems for communities established in the Watershed. Communicating Environmental Information to Promote Knowledge During fiscal 2015, the Panama Canal produced 50 radio programs, the environmental and community newspaper La Voz del Chagres, and testimonials, educational and environmental videos. It developed the ECOTIP, a new format of internal reporting on corporate achievements in terms of environmental management. In addition, with the youth environmentalist network, communication tools were imparted to the youngsters living in the Chilibre and Chilibrillo river areas and the Trans-isthmian Corridor to Colon. Specifically, they were trained on basic techniques of audiovisual production, and development of radio and television scripts, so that they can construct and communicate messages related to socio-environmental management and living conditions in their regions. Outputs from these trainings are used in radio, print and television media, several articles for La Voz del Chagres, videos and reports for Tu Canal, and the radio program La Cuenca al Día. 196 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 The Center for Environmental Information of the Canal Watershed (CIAC) continues collecting, analyzing, storing and disseminating environmental, social and economic information that is generated on the Canal Watershed, through different media and on the website of the institution. Climate Change The Panama Canal participates in the development of the National REDD (Reduced Emissions from Deforestation and Forest Degradation) Strategy, as a member of the National Committee on Climate Change. It also contributed in preparing the documents presented at the Twenty-First Conference of the Parties (COP) of the UN Framework Convention on Climate Change. Additionally, given the situation of historically low levels in the lakes of the Canal Watershed, and the effects of El Niño, the Panama Canal joined an effort coordinated by the Ministry of Environment to prepare the National Plan for Water Security (PNSH), which has a planning horizon that extends until 2050. At the same time, some of the measures taken by the Panama Canal during fiscal 2015 to offset the effects of El Niño were: • Temporary suspension of the hydroelectric generation. • Elimination of hydraulic assistance in the operation of the locks. • Cross-chamber filling to reduce the amount of water used for operations and combined lockages, including the transit of more than one ship in a single lock. • Continuous internal awareness campaign on water-saving initiatives that employees could practice at work, home and their communities. • Environmental education program in 153 schools, promoting good environmental practices on water saving and conservation as well as the protection of water resources of the Panama Canal Watershed. Water Resources Management in the Canal Watershed The Panama Canal has a unique hydro-meteorological data bank with over 100 years of information records collected from field stations. To preserve this information resource, the Canal has started transferring it to a more robust data base format called AQUARIUS. This will allow a user-friendly, more effective and efficient way for handling and processing information. The quality of data ensures Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 197 reliability of the information, which is published in hydrological and sediments yearbooks, reports on river flows, different thematic maps, and other information products. During fiscal 2015, the Water Division conducted critical works at Mendoza Water Treatment Plant. These included installing a protective mesh at the raw water intake to prevent the entry of aquatic vegetation into adduction pipes, removal of aquatic vegetation surrounding the raw water intake site, and manufacturing a pipeline bend for changing flow direction of pumped raw water. Staff of the Meteorology and Hydrology team participated in the V Forum on Climate Forecast Applications for Water Resources Management in Central America. The objective was to further strengthen regional capacities for the generation of hydrological forecasts and contribute to the reduction of climate risk for the water resources sector and adaptation to climate change. From July to September 2015, the Water Division carried out the Program for Continuous Hydrometeorological Conditions Surveillance in the Canal Watershed. Weekly meetings were held with the Canal Administrator and managers to evaluate and decide on possible establishment of draft restrictions due to low precipitations over the Watershed. As part of the Panama Canal Maintenance Program for Dams, Spillways and Auxiliary Dams, vertical sliding gates at Miraflores and Gatun spillways were reconditioned. Maintenance was also completed at Madden spillway drum gates. Miraflores Water Treatment Plant: 100 Years Producing Potable Water Personnel of the Miraflores Water Treatment Plant celebrated 100 years producing potable water for the Canal’s Pacific sector in March 2015. The plant was inaugurated on March 14, 1915, with an initial capacity of 56 million liters per day (MLD). Nowadays the plant produces 188 MLD to supply the cities of Panama and Arraijan, as well as the metropolitan areas of Casco Antiguo, San Felipe, Santa Ana, Chorrillo, Calidonia, Perejil, Avenida Nacional, Curundu and the Panama Canal operation areas at the Pacific side. These hundred years of efficient operation were achieved through an ongoing maintenance plan, innovations and constant improvements in many of the processes carried out to ensure production 198 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 of drinking water. During fiscal year 2015, the aeration tank was overhauled to continue this key treatment process for removing gases in the water. Another project was implemented for programming automatic chlorine dosage in raw water, which allows an adjusted control according to the raw water flow entering the plant. Upgrades to the water treatment filters at the Miraflores Plant also continued. Water Quality and Quantity The Panama Canal continues its Water Quality Surveillance and Monitoring Program. The Water Quality Index (ICA) is obtained by analyzing data on physical, chemical and biological elements, collected from stations at tributaries; priority Watersheds; and reservoirs Gatun, Miraflores, and Alhajuela. The ICA is an indicator of environmental performance and a tool for integrated water resource management. From October 2014 to September 2015, ICA was 87, on a scale of 0-100, which indicate that Canal reservoirs have good quality water. The Water Sampling Program collects and analyzes daily, monthly, quarterly and annual samples of several treatment processes, as well as the potable water delivered by the Canal’s three water treatment plants (Monte Esperanza, Miraflores and Mendoza). This Program, which was approved and is supervised by the National Authority of Public Services (ASEP), includes analysis of physical and chemical, bacteriological, metals, organic compounds, pesticides, algae, cyanobacteria, toxins, protozoa and other chemical and biological indicators. During fiscal year 2015, samples for the characterization and monitoring of oily water discharges, corporate waters and waters from the Canal floating equipment treatment systems were collected and analyzed to determine improvement measures. Raw Water Pumping Stations This fiscal year, an automatic chlorine gas dosing system for drinking water supplied to the community of Gamboa was completed. The new dosing system was installed in the vicinity of the Raw Water Pumping Station at Gamboa. This improves the chlorine dosing system for the area of Gamboa, helping to maintain the minimum permissible values of inorganic chemical characteristics for drinking water (residual chlorine) in accordance with the Technical Regulations COPANIT DGNTI-23-395-99. Water. Water, Definitions and General Requirements, Office of the Directorate General of Standards and Industrial Technology, Ministry of Commerce and Industry of the Republic of Panama. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 199 Chilled Water Plant The Balboa Chilled Water Plant replaced the cooling towers and engine control center. In addition, it is replacing the components of the cold water system that provide air conditioning service to Panama Canal buildings in Balboa. As part of the modernization plan, water pumps from cooling towers will be replaced. The work has improved efficiency in energy consumption per ton of cold water produced to 1.07 tons per kilowatt. This represents a reduction of energy consumption of 3,300 kilowatt hours on a daily average, compared with the consumption of previous years. The Panama Canal expects to change the main chillers with more efficient equipment in terms of tons per kilowatt ratio, and in terms of usage of more environmentally friendly refrigerants. Collection and Treatment of Wastewater in ACP Operational Areas The Panama Canal established a corporate initiative to implement processes of collection, treatment and purification in order to reduce wastewater in areas of operation by September 30, 2017 and manage the necessary actions to provide resources required to ensure the efficient operation of the new Sewerage and Wastewater Treatment Maintenance Unit. This initiative is also closely linked to compliance with local regulations and environmental policies, while it helps preserve the Panama Canal’s reputation as an environmentally-responsible company. Corporate Environmental Performance In fiscal year 2015, the Panama Canal Administrator, Deputy Administrator and Vice-Presidents signed the Memorandum of Decision for Environmental Commitment, which integrates the environmental component in all processes, programs, projects, objectives and goals to reduce the environmental impacts throughout the organization. The Environment Division continued to incorporate environmental elements in projects carried out at Canal operations areas; assessed environmental impact studies submitted by the Ministry of Environment regarding the development of projects in the Canal Watershed; and monitored compliance of environmental management plans (PMA) of companies located in the Watershed. The division also processed approval requests for projects to 200 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 be implemented in the Watershed; developed preliminary environmental assessments (EAP) for the development of projects in operational areas; and solved complaints about pollution of rivers and streams with wastewater discharges, unauthorized cutting of trees, and soil removal without proper permits, among others. Annual environmental inspections on safety and environmental protection were conducted in the following divisions: Infrastructure and Technology Operations (TIO), Engineering (IAI), Locks and Facilities Maintenance (OPE), Water (EAA), Purchasing, Warehousing and Inventories (FAA), Fleet and Equipment Maintenance (OPM), Transit Resources (OPR), Dredging (OPD), Energy (EAE) and Protection and Emergency Response (OPP). Compliance with Panama Canal environmental regulations were verified and different options were identified for continuous improvement in these operating areas. As a result of this program, best practices and continuous improvement measures were recommended and a reduction in oil leaks from locomotives was achieved. The Gatun Locks reached the highest percentage of loss reduction, followed by Pedro Miguel and Miraflores. Additionally, it was possible to reduce generation of oily water from tugboats by installing more efficient equipment. Awards were given to those whose environmental performance and contribution were outstanding: Locks and Facilities Maintenance Division (OPE), Water Division (EAA) and the crane ship Oceanus. Under the Effluent Monitoring Program, the Panama Canal continued to develop a baseline survey, which was launched in 2014 to verify the conditions of sanitation infrastructure, in line with the new sewerage and drainage systems projects implemented by the Canal. As part of the Monitoring Program for Total Waters, 135 environmental inspections were conducted to verify compliance of companies located in the trans-isthmian corridor with environmental requirements. Monitoring at the Miraflores Thermoelectric Plant proved a 100 percent compliance with Regulation 2610-EAC-1122 for point source emissions. With respect to mobile emission sources, road fleet results revealed a 94 percent compliance. Under the Corporate Environmental Education Program, the Panama Canal trained more than 400 employees on courses about environmental responsibility, held a tour to Miraflores Water Treatment Plant, offered talks on materials and a waste handling manual to personnel of the Dredging Division, 2Regulation for motor vehicles. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 201 Gatun and Pedro Miguel Locks, and offered talks to machines engineers under the MARPOL3 Convention for the prevention of pollution from ships. The Environment Division worked this year on the development of three regulations: a) activities for ship hull cleaning and maintenance work on deck; b) fuel transport; and c) management of ballast water in Canal waters, mainly in the anchorage area. Reliable, Continuous Power for the Canal The Energy Division remains at the forefront of energy generation to ensure an energy supply in the quality and continuity necessary for Canal operations, particularly for the transit of vessels. To fulfill this important mission, several projects have been carried out, such as changing generator governors at Gatun hydroelectric plant. This has allowed a quick response to electricity variations to which the power system is continuously exposed. This has secured the essential power supply for the operation of the Gatun Locks. Another project of great value to ensure continuity of power supply is replacing seven kilometers of 44 kilowatts power transmission towers from Balboa to Miraflores. Project implementation must ensure uninterrupted electricity in the areas of Balboa and Ancon, despite the unpredictable weather, and requires proper coordination with railway operation to ensure the safety of workers, passengers and avoid damages to third parties. Additionally, as an innovation project, the Energy Division is pioneering on the use of heavy fuel additives intended to produce energy in a more efficient way, resulting in lower production and maintenance costs, as well as reducing emissions to the atmosphere. 3The International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships. 202 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 Finance Financial Management The financial management of the Panama Canal is exercised in accordance to conservative and prudent policies, oriented at maintaining a competitive growth, preserve capital, provide sustainability for a strong operating margin, maintain appropriate cash reserves, generate returns above 14 percent on investments, as well as provide, in a timely manner, funds for the operation, maintenance and growth of the various Canal’s businesses. In order to increase contributions to the National Treasury, the Panama Canal maintains a permanent emphasis on improving productivity and cost efficiency. The Panama Canal’s liquidity is invested in short-term and investment grade instruments. The ACP operates its own revenue stream financing its capital expenditures at sustainable and higher than depreciation levels. Consequently, the Canal follows a conservative leverage policy designed to finance with external funds only major and strategic projects. The ACP is an independent legal entity under public law which operates under a clear and stable regulatory constitutional framework. Through the years under Panamanian administration, it has managed to consolidate a sustained solid, reliable and robust financial structure, committed to the administration, operation, conservation, maintenance and modernization of the Panama Canal and its related activities, so that the waterway may operate in a safe, continuous, efficient and cost effective manner. The Canal maintains continuous improvement through regular investments in modernization projects. A total of B/. 224.5 million were invested in fiscal year 2015. The total investment between fiscal year 2011 and 2015 totaled B/.1.268 billion. These investments include mooring stations, dredging channels and tugboats. Investments to Increase Value of the Maritime Route through the Canal The 2016-2022 strategy aims to attract more business from existing customers and routes of new customers to rapidly maximize the available capacity of the Third Set of Locks, which will begin operations in 2016. The new toll structure aims to address the customer’s needs per segment and includes a loyalty program which provides incentives to increase the use of the Panama route. In addition, the Canal will implement diversification initiatives, through concessions of business endeavors that offer synergies to the current business of transiting ships. This is aimed at strengthening the all-water route through Panama, by adding value through other services, and increase Canal transits, especially of Neo Panamax vessels. PROJECT DIVERSIFICATION PORTFOLIO Corozal Container LNG Terminal Terminal Roll-On Roll-Off Terminal Pipelines Logistics Parks Shipyard Services Top-Off Fuel Bunkering Operations Energy Generation Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 207 Diversification in logistics and maritime-related services will make the Panama Canal route more attractive, improving transit levels, the overall value and the demand for services that are regularly offered to customers. Inaugural Capital Markets Bond Issue In addition to the construction of the Third Set of Locks, the ACP also acquired in 2008 by law the responsibility to build a vehicular crossing over the Canal on the Atlantic side. Fulfilling this responsibility, the ACP started in June 2011 the design and construction of a 4.605-km-long modern bridge connecting the west to the east side of the Canal at its northern end. In March 2015, the ACP Board of Directors approved an inaugural bond issue in the international capital markets to complete the financing for the construction of the bridge estimated at B/.570 million. At the end fiscal year 2015, the bridge construction already had a 42 percent progress, funded internally with B/.120 million. In September 2015, the ACP successfully executed its first bond issue for B/.450 million, maturing in 2035, at a annual rate of 4.95 percent and an effective rate of 5.14 percent per year, payable semi-annually beginning January 2016 until July 2035. This bond issue was executed under SEC Rule 144/ Regulation S to qualified institutional investors. In fiscal year 2015, the ACP was rated investment grade A2 by Moody’s, Aby Standard & Poor’s and A by Fitch Ratings which, at the end of fiscal year 2015, are 2 and 3 notches above the sovereign rating. ACP’s recognized strengths by the rating agencies included: • Autonomous legal entity under public law established under a Constitutional Title XIV (1972) and the Organic Law 19 of 1997. • With its own patrimony and the right to administer it. • It is exclusively responsible for the management, operation, conservation, maintenance and modernization of the Canal, as well as its related activities and services in order to operate safely, uninterruptedly, efficiently and profitable. • It is responsible of undertaking all operating costs, investment, operation, maintenance, modernization, expansion of the Canal and required reserves as determined by the Board of Directors. • Investments of funds must be short-term and in investment grade investments. 208 Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 The recognition of international investors under the independent assessment of the rating agencies, in addition to its historical financial performance demonstrated during the 16 years of Panamanian administration, translated into an oversubscription of offers of up to four times above the indicative needs of B/.450 million. Financial Performance for Fiscal Year 2015 Fiscal year 2015 ended with total revenues of B/.2.610 billion, which corresponds to B/.18.9 million or 0.7 percent below fiscal year 2014. This variant in revenue is primarily due to a 58.8 percent decline in the sale of electricity2 and lower fuel costs of 52.5 percent over previous year. Total expenditures for fiscal year 2015 of B/.1.249 billion were 4.2 percent or B/.54.4 million below fiscal year 2014. As a result, earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization (EBITDA) of B/.1.453 billion were above 2.8 percent of fiscal year 2014. Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 209 Revenues REVENUE DISTRIBUTION 1.5% 5.0% The Panama Canal’s main source of revenues comes from transit services, which include tolls 93.5% and other marine services, which amounted to B/.2.441 billion for fiscal year 2015 equivalent to 93.5 percent of total revenues. Sale of electricity and potable water represented five percent and the remaining 1.5 percent comes from interests and other revenues. Transit Energy and Water Other Toll revenues of B/.1.994 billion registered an increase of 4.4 percent over fiscal 2014, driven by a record tonnage of 340.8 million PC/UMS 97, surpassing by 2.1 percent the record achieved in fiscal year 2012 of 333.7 million PC/UMS 97 tons. The increase in toll revenues compensated for lower revenues from the sale of electricity, exceeding the budget revenue by B/.194.3 million, equivalent to 8.0 percent. Revenues from other maritime services represent 17.1 percent of total revenues, contributing B/.446.7 million on fiscal 2015. This category had an increase by B/.33 million (8.0 percent) over the previous year, due to increments in the daily transit reservations and transit slot auctions (13.2 percent), as well as services such as pilotage (60.7 percent), tugboats (6.0 percent) and lanches (4.1 percent). This is mainly attributed to the fact that higher-tonnage ships require more Canal ressources. Growth in transit tonnage, mainly by containers en route from Asia, was largely due to the repositioning of routes and ships due to congestion at the US TRANSIT REVENUES West Coast ports caused by labor negotiations 3.000 strikes. Fiscal Year Budget Real Surplus Minimum Surplus Panama Canal / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 215 Financial Indicators The Panama Canal’s financial profitability indicators remain strong, achieving more than 50 cents of net income per balboa. This is the result of the Canal’s efficient operation and the excellent use of the geographical position of the all-water route through Panama, which allowed the Canal to generate wealth, even with low levels of global growth and an uncertain economic situation worldwide. Asset turnover registered a decline of two percentage points from 22.5 percent in the construction in progress category, associated with the Expansion Program. Return on total assets fell 1 percentage point as a result of constructions in progress during the execution of the Expansion Program. The capital multiplier increase is a result of the issuance of bonds for B/.450 million as part of the financing for the construction of bridge in the Atlantic side of the Canal. The return on equity of 16 percent highlights the Canal’s efficient capital management of investments, validating its ability to generate value in a sustainable manner. Impairment of non-financial assets Provision for marine accidents and other claims The ACP assesses, at each reporting period date, whether there is an indication that an asset may be impaired. If any indication exists, The ACP recognizes a provision for marine accidents and contract the ACP estimates the asset’s recoverable amount, defined as the claims as soon as a known probable economic obligation is derived higher of an asset’s fair value less costs to sell and its value in use. from any particular incident. When the asset´s carrying amount exceeds its recoverable value, the asset is consider as impaired and it is adjusted to its recoverable value. In assessing value in use, the estimated future cash flows are discounted to their present value using a pre-tax discount rate that reflects current market assessments of the time value of money and the risk specific to the asset. Impairment losses are recognized in the income statement in the year they are determined. 10 Canal de Panama / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 227 Autoridad del Canal de Panama Notes to Financial Statements September 30, 2015 (In t h o u sa n d s o f balboas B /.) 3. Contributed capital Article 316 of the Political Constitution of the Republic of Panama states that the ACP has its own patrimony and the right to manage it. Upon the transfer of the Canal to the Republic of Panama at noon on December 31, 1999, the ACP became the administrator of all goods and real estate property identified in the Organic Law of the ACP as the patrimony necessary to operate and maintain the Canal. This patrimony is divided into two groups: the inalienable patrimony, comprised of land, lakes, rivers, dams, locks and anchorages, as established in Article 2 of the Organic Law; and the economic patrimony, comprised of all those installations, buildings, structures and equipment that support the operation of the Canal, as established in Article 33 of the same Law. 21 238 Canal de Panama / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 Autoridad del Canal de Panama Notes to Financial Statements September 30, 2015 ( I n t h o u sa nds of balboas B /.) 13. Reserves and contributions for investment programs and inventories Changes in reserves and contributions are detailed as follows: Canal expansion The ACP maintains an equity reserve for the construction program of the Panama Canal third set of locks. The funds for this reserve are segregated based on the levels of earnings obtained, according to the financing needs of the ACP for determined projects during the execution of the Program. In fiscal 2015, the reserve was used in order to complete the financing required for the expansion program. Catastrophic risks The ACP maintains an equity reserve to cover the deductibles of the catastrophic risks insurance policies with a maximum amount of B/.36,000. Contingencies and working capital The ACP maintains an equity reserve for contingencies and working capital which is calculated based on the ACP´s level of revenues and is defined as 30 days of average revenues or billing of the Canal. During fiscal year 2015, it was approved to decrease this reserve by B/.1,620 while in 2014, it was approved to increased it by B/.18,300 for a total reserve of B/.213,510 (2014: B/.215,130). Enterprise capitalization The ACP maintains an equity reserve for capitalization with the purpose to ensure and facilitate the long-term financial projection of the ACP. During fiscal year 2015, it was approved to increase this reserve by B/.13,226 while in 2014, it was approved to decreased it by B/.3,105 for a total reserve of B/.274,969 (2014: B/.261,743). Strategic for investment programs In fiscal year 2014, the ACP established an equity reserve for B/.150,000 to maintain strategic sustainability and competitiveness of the Canal, ahead ensuring the availability of funds to meet additional needs of existing investment projects and to take advantage of growth opportunities requiring the implementation of new investment projects. In fiscal year 2015, the reserve was of B/.240,000 (2014: B/.150,000). 22 Canal de Panama / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 239 Autoridad del Canal de Panama Notes to Financial Statements September 30, 2015 (In t h o u sa n d s o f balboas B /.) 13. Employees benefits (continued) Reimbursement right to ACP The ACP contracted a reimbursement policy, in accordance with IAS 19, to cover the defined benefit plans for voluntary retirement of employees. The policy ensures the ACP reimbursement of all payments made by the ACP in respect of defined benefit plans for voluntary retirement of employees during the term of the plan as long as the ACP makes annual installments to the insurance company as a guarantee deposit equal to the probable amount that the ACP would pay during the year for the retirement benefit plans. In addition, the reimbursement policy provides protection in each year of its term against the risk that the ACP suffers any financially incapacitating event to meet payment of obligations to its employees, for any reason, including illiquidity, if occurred during the term of the policy, as long as the ACP is current in the payments of the premium and the defined benefit plans for voluntary retirement are in effect. The policy does not cover the risk of default of the ACP that could arise from internal fraud, catastrophic physical risks, nuclear war, terrorism, and epidemics, which has been estimated at 3.41% of the total insured amount. Changes in the reimbursement right to ACP during fiscal year 2015 are detailed as follows: At September 30, 2015, the ACP paid the insurer B/.20,248 (2014: B/.1,065) in premiums of the reimbursement policy. 25. Risk management The ACP maintains a conservative and prudent financial policy oriented to preserve its capital and generate optimal performance with low risk, for which various risk management activities are performed throughout the year, including: analysis, evaluation and risk mitigation. This allows management to plan and make decisions that enhance the economic contribution and operational excellence, improving the chances of achieving the strategic goals. The ACP’s capital structure consists of net debt (borrowings and bonds as detailed in notes 16 and 17), compensated by cash and bank deposit balances, other cost amortized financial assets (See note 9) and equity of the enterprise (consisting of contributed capital, reserves, other equity accounts, and unappropriated retained earnings, as disclosed in notes 12, 13, 14 and 15, respectively). Historically, the ACP has operated with its own resources due to its high levels of cash generated by its operations to afford its costs of operation, investment, functioning, maintenance and modernization. However, on December 9, 2008 the ACP subscribed to a common term financing agreement with five development agencies, which committed to grant borrowings totaling B/.2,300 millions with the purpose of partially finance the Canal Expansion Program, and more recently, in September 2015, when issued bonds in the international market by B/.450 millions in order to partially finance the building of the bridge on the Atlantic side of the Canal. (See note 17) 29 246 Canal de Panama / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 Autoridad del Canal de Panama Notes to Financial Statements September 30, 2015 ( I n t h o u sa nds of balboas B /.) 25. Risk management (continued) Categories of financial instruments Financial risk management objectives ACP’s main financial liability consists of borrowings, bonds payable and trade accounts payable. The main purpose of these financial liabilities is to finance the Canal Expansion Program and the new bridge at the Atlantic side of the Canal. The ACP also has cash, bank deposits, operations with settlement in progress, trade and other receivables, and funds invested in debt instruments with short maturities which are held until maturity. The ACP also contracts hedging instruments. The ACP is exposed to credit, market and liquidity risks. The ACP administration monitors the management of these risks. ACP’s Treasury coordinates the access to international financial markets, monitors and manages the financial risks related to the ACP’s operations through internal risk reports, which it is analyzed the exposures depending on their degree and magnitude. These risks include market risk (including exchange risk and price risk), credit risk, liquidity risk, and interest rate risk. All the activities related to risk hedging are performed by teams of specialists with the appropriate knowledge, experience and supervision. 30 Canal de Panama / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 247 Autoridad del Canal de Panama Notes to Financial Statements September 30, 2015 (In t h o u sa n d s o f balboas B /.) 25. Risk management (continued) Fair value of financial instruments measured at amortized cost Except for what is detailed in the following table, the ACP considers that the carrying amounts of financial assets and financial liabilities recognized at amortized cost in the financial statements approximate their fair values: Valuation techniques and assumptions applied in order to measure fair value The fair value of financial assets and financial liabilities is determined in the following manner: • The fair value of financial assets and financial liabilities with standard terms and conditions and traded on active liquid markets is determined with reference to quoted market prices. • The fair value of other financial assets and financial liabilities (excluding hedging instruments) is determined in accordance with generally accepted fixed pricing models based on discounted cash flow analysis using prices from current observed market transactions and quotes for similar instruments. • The fair value of derivative instruments is calculated using quoted prices. When such prices are not available, a discounted cash flow analysis is performed using the applicable yield curve for the duration of the instruments for non-optional derivatives, and option pricing models for optional derivatives. Interest rate swaps are measured at the present value of future cash flows estimated and discounted based on the applicable yield curves derived from quoted interest rates. Fair value measurements recognized in the statement of financial position The following table provides an analysis of financial instruments that are measured subsequently to initial recognition at fair value, grouped into levels from 1 to 3 based on the degree to which the fair value is observed: • Level 1: fair value measurements are those derived from quoted prices (unadjusted) in active markets for identical assets or liabilities. • Level 2: fair value measurements are those derived from indicators other than quoted prices included within Level 1 which are observable for the assets or liabilities either directly (i.e. as prices) or indirectly (i.e. derived from prices). • Level 3: fair value measurements are those derived from valuation techniques that include indicators for the assets or liabilities that are not based on observable market data (unobservable indicators). 35 252 Canal de Panama / ANNUAL REPORT 2015 Autoridad del Canal de Panama Notes to Financial Statements September 30, 2015 ( I n t h o u sa nds of balboas B /.) 25.
